                            <div class="player-info-box-header team-astros"><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>George Springer</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team"><a href="/teams/astros">Houston Astros</a></div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 28</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: R/R</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-item">6' 3" / 215</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-pos">OF</div></div>
                        <div class="player-info-bio">
                            <strong>Birthdate: </strong>9/19/1989 (28 y, 5 m, 19 d)<br /><b>Drafted:</b> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=12856&position=OF#draft" style="text-decoration:none;">2011 June Amateur Draft - Round: 1, Pick: 11, Overall: 11, Team: Houston Astros</a><br /><strong>Contract:</strong> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=12856&position=OF#contract" >$3.9M / 1 Years (2017)</a>
                        </div>
                    </td>

                    
                    <tr><td><hr class="player-page-info-sep"/><div class="player-page-rotowire"><strong><a href="https://www.rotowire.com/users/ad_jump.asp?id=688&playerid=12130">RotoWire News</a>:</strong> Springer avoided arbitration with the Astros by signing a two-year, $24 million deal Monday, Bob Nightengale of USA Today reports. (2/5/2018)</div></td></tr>

            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        
        
        
        
        
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>While there are probably major leaguers who swing with <i>equal</i> vigor, there aren't any who swing with <i>more</i> of it than George Springer. He's enthusiastic, is Springer, about hitting the ball with authority -- and that enthusiasm has translated to success. For the most part, at least. Over parts of four minor-league seasons, Springer averaged 31 home runs per 600 plate appearances -- a pace that would have placed him among the major-league leaders over that interval. Indeed, among major leaguers who recorded at least 300 plate appearances in 2014, Springer recorded the sixth-highest home-run rate (35 HR per 600 PA), right between Giancarlo Stanton and Jose Abreu by that measure. Contact, however, both has been and will likely continue to be an issue for Springer. Ultimately, he profiles as an above-average player anyway, -- with his capacity to draw walks, his speed, and his outfield defense compensating for his one clear flaw. Of some interest beyond that, perhaps, is how his footspeed manifests itself over a full major-league season. Having stolen 45 bases (in just 53 attempts) as recently as 2013, Springer recorded only 11 steals across all levels in 2014. It wouldn't be surprising to see him post a total closer to that latter figure than the former in 2015 given the steep age curve for speed. (Carson Cistulli)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Although he's recorded only 300-something plate appearances, there's no one in the majors who exhibits decisively <i>more</i> power on contact than Springer. Provided he earns enough walks to compensate for his lack of contact ability, he profiles as an above-average player.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Tools. Whatever adjective you choose to append -- oozing, dripping, gushing -- there's no question Springer has tools. The power is devastating, the swing is violently delectable, and he abuses mistakes. Springer improved in 2015 even as he regressed in predictable ways. He posted an unreal 28% home run per fly ball ratio in 2014. That declined to a sane -- but still excellent -- 19% HR/FB in 2015. As for the improvement, he showed off his wheels more frequently and cut his strikeout rate from 33% to 24%. The 2015 season was one of adjustments for Springer, and he appears poised for 2016 breakout. Naysayers can point to any number of exciting prospects who tantalized but never quite put the superstar skills together. Justin Upton comes to mind. There was always a feeling he could do more. Springer's also older than most former elite prospects -- he's entering his age 26 season. Injuries have prevented him from compiling a full major league campaign. Hopefully he can stay healthy in 2016. (Brad Johnson)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Few players promise more upside than Springer. His combination of power, speed, and selectivity teases a superstar ceiling. Alfonso Soriano with more walks (and fewer steals) is one possible outcome. However, there's always a risk that he never puts it all together.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Springer’s season was essentially a classic compiler season. He leveraged his MLB-leading 744 plate appearances into a fancy little fantasy season, but despite nearly 300 more plate appearances over 2015, he was worth just 0.7 more wins above replacement. All three of his triple slash figures (AVG/OBP/SLG) were down, though he had modest gains in his strikeout and walk rates. He failed to reach double-digit stolen bases with just a 9-for-19 success rate (47%) and it’s unclear if he will work to change that much in 2017 <a href="https://soundcloud.com/siriusxmfantasy/george-springer-says-hell-only-steal-bases-when-the-situation-says-im-supposed-to">based on some early-March comments</a>. On the other hand, let’s not sleep on a .359 OBP atop a strong lineup that led to 116 runs and should yield another triple-digit total in 2017. I think the real question should be about how much more power remains, not if he will get his steals back. He hits the ball relatively hard (39th of 117 with 35% Hard% since 2014, min. 1500 PA), has a selective eye (21st with 12% BB rate), and consistently makes the most of his flyball rate (5th with 21% HR/FB rate). He was just a homer away from 30 and something around 35 is a reachable upside. He rarely falls out of the top 50 picks, which makes sense. (<a href="http://twitter.com/sporer">Paul Sporer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Springer was a volume compiler in 2016, but that still takes skill and it's not like he didn't show big time potential in 2014-15. Speed one of those things that can flip year-to-year, but Springer himself has suggested he won't be necessarily belookingto run given the excellent lineup behind him. He's not cheap, but he's still really good and at 27, we haven't necessarily seen the best of him.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

He was no Mike Trout, but as a 4-WAR player with 30-homer power, any franchise would have already been happy to build around him. He took the next step anyway, boosting his <abbr title=\"contact rate\">Contact%</abbr> several percentage points, thus pushing his <abbr title=\"strikeout rate\">K%</abbr> below 20% for the first time. It fueled a batting average surge and, in tandem with his first 30-homer season, made Springer a bona fide star.  Springer’s contact gains are part of an informal annual tradition: he continues to achieve remarkable gains in contact on pitches in the zone year over year. We often perceive power and contact as inversely related, such that a player must sacrifice contact to tap into his power and vice versa. Alas, it’s all the more incredible Springer ramped up his power in his best season by contact.  It’s safer to assume everything regresses a bit in 2018 – maybe the K% climbs and the power recedes a bit. Yet there’s a small chance his contact skills improve <i>yet again</i>, making him, what, a center-fielding Nolan Arenado? Can you imagine? Springer is entrenched as a top-10 outfielder with 40-homer upside and an outside shot to bat .300 if his <abbr title=\"batting average on balls in play\">BABIP</abbr> breaks right.
